      <abstract>
        <p>Brucellosis is considered one of the most hazardous zoonotic diseases all over the world. It causes formidable economic losses in developed and developing countries. Despite the significant attempts to get rid of <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens in many parts of the world, the disease continues to spread widely. Recently, many attempts proved to be effective for the prevention and control of highly contagious bovine brucellosis, which could be followed by others to achieve a prosperous future without rampant <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens. In this study, the updated view for worldwide <italic>Brucella</italic> distribution, possible predisposing factors for emerging <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens, immune response and different types of <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccines, genomics and proteomics approaches incorporated recently in the field of brucellosis, and future perspectives for prevention and control of bovine brucellosis have been discussed comprehensively. So, the current study will be used as a guide for researchers in planning their future work, which will pave the way for a new world without these highly contagious pathogens that have been infecting and threatening the health of humans and terrestrial animals.</p>
      </abstract>

  <body>
    <sec sec-type="intro" id="sec1">
      <label>1.</label>
      <title>Introduction</title>
      <p>Brucellosis is a zoonotic infection caused by intracellular <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens (<xref rid="ref1" ref-type="bibr">1</xref>). Twelve species are now considered in the genus <italic>Brucella</italic> that infect different domestic and wildlife animal species (<xref rid="ref2" ref-type="bibr">2</xref>). Among them, six <italic>Brucella</italic> species have been classified according to their pathogenicity and natural hosts as <italic>Brucella</italic> (<italic>B.</italic>) <italic>abortus</italic> (cattle), <italic>B. melitensis</italic> (sheep and goats), <italic>B. suis</italic> (pigs), <italic>B. canis</italic> (dogs), <italic>B. ovis</italic> (rams), and <italic>B. neotomae</italic> (Common voles and desert wood rat) (<xref rid="ref3" ref-type="bibr">3</xref>, <xref rid="ref4" ref-type="bibr">4</xref>). <italic>B. melitensis</italic> and <italic>B. abortus</italic> are the most significant species in animals and humans (<xref rid="ref5" ref-type="bibr">5</xref>). Later, two new <italic>Brucella</italic> spp., namely, <italic>B. pinnipedialis</italic> (walruses and seals) and <italic>B. ceti</italic> (dolphins, porpoises, and whales), have been reported to infect marine mammals (<xref rid="ref6" ref-type="bibr">6</xref>). Brucellosis is a major health concern for animals because it causes significant economic losses in developing countries across the world in the form of abortion, stillbirth, delayed conception, retained placenta, reduced milk production, contagious epididymitis, and the cost of treatment and productivity loss (<xref rid="ref7" ref-type="bibr">7</xref>) (<xref rid="tab1" ref-type="table">Table 1</xref>). Abortion and infertility are the most common clinical symptoms of animal brucellosis, and they are not specific in nature. Abortion always occurs during the first pregnancy, but it is less commonly present after that due to persistent immunity (<xref rid="ref24" ref-type="bibr">24</xref>). In addition, Xie et al. proved that there are three parameters, including animal species, vaccination dose, and immunization route, that were statistically linked to the occurrence of abortion as an adverse reaction to <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccination (<xref rid="ref25" ref-type="bibr">25</xref>). Since it is easily transmissible by exposure to aborted fetuses, placentae, vaginal fluids, and milk, congenitally and/or venereally, the disease is very contagious in all circumstances (<xref rid="ref26" ref-type="bibr">26</xref>).</p>

          </tbody>
        </table>
      </table-wrap>
      <p>Human infections mostly occur after contact with infected cattle, sheep, goats, and camels. In humans, brucellosis is characterized by a variety of non-specific symptoms such as malaise, lethargy, arthritis, and fever. These symptoms are mostly chronic lasting for years, and treatment needs to ensure compliance with a costly and prolonged therapy (<xref rid="ref27" ref-type="bibr">27</xref>). It also can be manifested as a neurological disorder (<xref rid="ref28" ref-type="bibr">28</xref>). People can get the infection by contact with infected animals during their usual work (occupational) as in the case of veterinarians, abattoir workers, and livestock keepers. Consumption of unpasteurized milk is also regarded as a common source of infection (<xref rid="ref29" ref-type="bibr">29</xref>). Although pasteurizing milk kills <italic>Brucella</italic> and prevents infection in humans, it is not commonly performed in some communities with a widespread lack of public awareness about the danger of drinking raw milk (<xref rid="ref30" ref-type="bibr">30</xref>). In addition, human-to-human transmission was reported via breastfeeding, hardly through sexual intercourse, blood transfusions, organ transplantation, and transplacentally (<xref rid="ref31" ref-type="bibr">31</xref>). <italic>Brucella</italic> infections are more likely to be prevented and controlled when immunization is combined with adequate preventive measures (<xref rid="ref32" ref-type="bibr">32</xref>).</p>
      <p>As a result of increasing the severity of brucellosis, in this study, we described its global distribution and possible predisposing factors that facilitate the infection&#x2019;s spreading. The immune response and how <italic>Brucella</italic> can evade the host immune system, as well as the recent trends and advances in the diagnosis, vaccination, treatment, prevention, and control of bovine brucellosis, have been discussed comprehensively. Subsequently, this review can be used as a guide for veterinarians and health workers to stop the further spread of this contagion all over the world.</p>
    </sec>
    <sec id="sec2">
      <label>2.</label>
      <title>Brucellosis worldwide</title>
      <p>Brucellosis is one of the most common zoonotic diseases that increase poverty levels in poor nations (<xref rid="ref1" ref-type="bibr">1</xref>). It has a negative influence on the world economy and public health. Annually, from 5 to 12.5 million human cases of brucellosis are reported, making it one of the most serious zoonoses on the globe (<xref rid="ref33" ref-type="bibr">33</xref>). Human <italic>Brucella</italic> seroprevalence has been reported in many countries all over the world, especially among a variety of highly occupational groups as follows: China (15.5%) (<xref rid="ref34" ref-type="bibr">34</xref>); India (1.83%) (<xref rid="ref35" ref-type="bibr">35</xref>); Pakistan (17%) (<xref rid="ref36" ref-type="bibr">36</xref>); Malaysia (5.4%) (<xref rid="ref37" ref-type="bibr">37</xref>); Saudi Arabia (33.9%) (<xref rid="ref38" ref-type="bibr">38</xref>); Greece (3%) (<xref rid="ref39" ref-type="bibr">39</xref>); Egypt (31.3%) (<xref rid="ref40" ref-type="bibr">40</xref>); South Sudan (33.3%) (<xref rid="ref41" ref-type="bibr">41</xref>); Nigeria (17.6%) (<xref rid="ref42" ref-type="bibr">42</xref>); Cameroon (5.6%) (<xref rid="ref43" ref-type="bibr">43</xref>); Kenya (32.3%) (<xref rid="ref44" ref-type="bibr">44</xref>); Uganda (17%) (<xref rid="ref45" ref-type="bibr">45</xref>), and Tanzania (1.41%) (<xref rid="ref46" ref-type="bibr">46</xref>) (<xref rid="SM1" ref-type="supplementary-material">Supplementary Table S1</xref>).</p>
      <p>The annual incidence rate of human brucellosis is between 0.5/100,000 and 70/100,000, depending on the area of study (<xref rid="ref47" ref-type="bibr">47</xref>), while the prevalence of brucellosis in animals ranges from 0.2 to 20%, depending on the location and species (<xref rid="ref48" ref-type="bibr">48</xref>) (<xref rid="SM1" ref-type="supplementary-material">Supplementary Table S1</xref>). Azerbaijan is now ranked 13th in the world for the incidence of human brucellosis, with an estimated yearly incidence of over 50 cases per million (<xref rid="ref49" ref-type="bibr">49</xref>). Human brucellosis has recently been linked to raw milk consumption and the RB51 vaccination status (<xref rid="ref50" ref-type="bibr">50</xref>, <xref rid="ref51" ref-type="bibr">51</xref>). Therefore, the raw milk movement in the United States has been controlled, and numerous states have approved legislation that restricts the selling of raw milk and raw milk products to customers. Nowadays, less than 100 cases are recorded annually in the United States, with the majority coming in the South and Southwest from illegally imported soft cheeses (unpasteurized) from Mexico (<xref rid="ref52" ref-type="bibr">52</xref>). In Tanzania, the upfront cost of pasteurization facilities is now unaffordable; hence, alternative management techniques are regarded as more cost-effective. In a Tanzanian investigation, 56% of the inspected milk samples (<italic>n</italic> =&#x2009;59) tested positive for brucellosis (<xref rid="ref53" ref-type="bibr">53</xref>).</p>
      <p><italic>Brucella</italic> is regarded as a spillover organism that begins to affect other species suddenly. From this point, cattle can be infected with both <italic>B. melitensis</italic> and <italic>B. abortus</italic> (<xref rid="ref49" ref-type="bibr">49</xref>). In Azerbaijan, milk and blood samples were collected during early lactation in farms with a history of abortions and previous seropositive results for brucellosis. Out of 57 milk samples collected from seropositive cows, 22 samples revealed microbial growth on Farrell&#x2019;s media with 5% CO2 incubation. On the other hand, 8 milk samples revealed growth in the absence of CO2. According to biotyping classification, these 22 and 8 samples were classified as <italic>B. abortus</italic> and <italic>B. melitensis</italic>, respectively. By using multiple-locus variable number tandem repeat analysis (MLVA) and matching the two types of <italic>Brucella</italic>, <italic>B. abortus</italic> strains were matched with <italic>B. abortus</italic> strains isolated in East Europe, Central Asia, and China. <italic>B. melitensis</italic> strains had a new genotype circulating among cattle, sheep, and goats in Azerbaijan, and these strains belong to the American clade that is hardly identified in the region (<xref rid="ref49" ref-type="bibr">49</xref>). In Mongolia, seropositive camel herds were closely associated with cattle infection (<xref rid="ref54" ref-type="bibr">54</xref>). In China, the total prevalence of bovine brucellosis was 1.9% according to a meta-analysis conducted over a 10-year period (2008&#x2013;2018). Northern China has a higher prevalence than Southern China. More specifically, Jilin province has the highest prevalence of more than 30% (<xref rid="ref55" ref-type="bibr">55</xref>). During previous Pakistani research, RBPT analysis of cow and buffalo samples revealed that 170 serum samples (6.3%) and 47 herds (18.6%) were seropositive for brucellosis (<xref rid="ref56" ref-type="bibr">56</xref>). In a recent meta-analysis of animal diseases in North Eastern India, bovine brucellosis was shown to be prevalent in 17% of cattle (<xref rid="ref57" ref-type="bibr">57</xref>). In addition, in India, Pathak et al. examined 481 samples from 296 animals, including milk, blood, vaginal swabs, vaginal discharges, placental tissue, and fetal tissues. Of these samples, 30.4 and 41.6% were positive for brucellosis using RBPT and iELISA, respectively, whereas 27.0% were seropositive by both tests (<xref rid="ref58" ref-type="bibr">58</xref>). In domestic animals, only Canada, the United States, Japan, Australia, New Zealand, and Western and Central European countries are almost free of both <italic>B. abortus</italic> and <italic>B. melitensis</italic> (<xref rid="ref59" ref-type="bibr">59</xref>).</p>
      <p>On the other hand, the disease has a devastating impact on Africa, the Middle East, Latin America, Eastern Europe, and large parts of Asia (<xref rid="ref60" ref-type="bibr">60</xref>). In Jordan, Musallam et al. estimated seroprevalence levels of 18.1% in cow herds (<xref rid="ref61" ref-type="bibr">61</xref>). In Egypt, the prevalence frequency varies greatly across the country (<xref rid="ref62" ref-type="bibr">62</xref>). <italic>B. melitensis</italic>, <italic>B. abortus</italic>, and <italic>B. suis</italic> are the three zoonotic <italic>Brucella</italic> species found in Egypt. <italic>B. melitensis</italic> biovar 3 is the most frequent <italic>Brucella</italic> isolate in Egypt right now in both livestock and people (<xref rid="ref63" ref-type="bibr">63</xref>). Hosein et al. studied the outbreak in buffalo caused by <italic>B. melitensis</italic> biovar 3. The study revealed that being free of brucellosis required 6 months, which is considered a long time for transmitting infection to other areas, particularly in unhygienic conditions and husbandry systems that allow mixed populations of different ages and sexes and abortion with a lack of controlled animal movement (<xref rid="ref64" ref-type="bibr">64</xref>). A novel <italic>Brucella</italic> strain closely related to <italic>B. melitensis</italic> biovar 3 was identified in Croatian cattle during testing within a brucellosis eradication program (<xref rid="ref65" ref-type="bibr">65</xref>). Chaka et al. discovered that the herd-level prevalence of brucellosis in Ethiopian cattle was 32%, while an overall cattle-level prevalence of 9.7% was recorded dependent on serological tests (<xref rid="ref66" ref-type="bibr">66</xref>). Moreover, Getachew et al. tested 278 serum samples for brucellosis in dairy animals in Ethiopia by RBPT, indirect ELISA (iELISA), and complement fixation test (CFT). The sensitivity was reported as 89.6, 96.8, and 94%, respectively, and the specificity was reported as 84.5, 96.3, and 88.5%, respectively. Comparing the three tests, iELISA was shown to have the highest sensitivity and specificity (<xref rid="ref67" ref-type="bibr">67</xref>).</p>
      <p>In Abuja, Nigeria, Aworh et al. tested 376 cattle for <italic>Brucella</italic> infection. Twenty-one animals were RBPT positive, and two animals gave positive competitive ELISA (cELISA) results (<xref rid="ref68" ref-type="bibr">68</xref>). Using the milk ring test and iELISA, Kamwine et al. reported a prevalence of 26.5% of <italic>Brucella</italic> in 185 raw milk samples in Uganda (<xref rid="ref69" ref-type="bibr">69</xref>). In Tanzania, milk samples were used for iELISA-based <italic>Brucella</italic> surveillance, and the study revealed a herd frequency of 44.4% (<xref rid="ref70" ref-type="bibr">70</xref>). In Ghana, blood samples were collected from 315 cattle and 178 cattle farmers to measure <italic>Brucella</italic> seroprevalence, and the RBPT results demonstrated 22.9 and 10.1% in bovines and humans, respectively (<xref rid="ref71" ref-type="bibr">71</xref>). Madut et al. demonstrated a high prevalence level of brucellosis in cattle and their owners in Bahr El-Ghazal, Sudan (<xref rid="ref41" ref-type="bibr">41</xref>). In Ecuador, brucellosis seroprevalence and risk variables have been investigated in dairy and dairy-beef mixed cattle herds, and the results indicated that the true <italic>Brucella</italic> seropositivity was 17.0% (<xref rid="ref72" ref-type="bibr">72</xref>) (<xref rid="fig1" ref-type="fig">Figure 1</xref>).</p>
      <fig position="float" id="fig1">
        <label>Figure 1</label>
        <caption>
          <p>The effect of brucellosis on animals.</p>
        </caption>
        <graphic xlink:href="fvets-10-1255239-g001" position="float"/>
      </fig>
    </sec>
    <sec id="sec3">
      <label>3.</label>
      <title>Risk and predisposing factors for bovine brucellosis</title>
      <p>Common risk factors related to the difficulty of brucellosis control are social and political instabilities and inappropriate diagnosis, reporting, and application of control measures. Livestock husbandry systems and their interactions with other animals and wildlife, as well as using unpasteurized dairy products as one of the traditional cultural habits, played a major role in transmitting brucellosis (<xref rid="ref32" ref-type="bibr">32</xref>, <xref rid="ref73" ref-type="bibr">73&#x2013;75</xref>) (<xref rid="fig2" ref-type="fig">Figure 2</xref>).</p>
      <fig position="float" id="fig2">
        <label>Figure 2</label>
        <caption>
          <p>The risk and predisposing factors of brucellosis.</p>
        </caption>
        <graphic xlink:href="fvets-10-1255239-g002" position="float"/>
      </fig>
      <p>The outbreaks of brucellosis in animals are increasingly important nowadays because of its asymptomatic features in humans. The follow-up outcomes of asymptomatic brucellosis without treatment were investigated in 3,610 studies from 1990 to 2021. Of which, 13 studies included that during a 0.5- to 18-month follow-up period, 40.3% of the cases remained asymptomatic. Moreover, investigations on brucellosis transmission vary between different geographies and populations (<xref rid="ref76" ref-type="bibr">76</xref>). Moreover, many studies have detected substantial correlations among species, gender, age, and breed of animals with seropositivity (<xref rid="ref77" ref-type="bibr">77&#x2013;79</xref>). Lack of clean water, insufficient manure collection, neglected cleaning, bad management of aborted materials, the introduction of new animals from infected herds or herds with unclear status, and mixed herds are among the other risk factors that have been highlighted (<xref rid="ref80" ref-type="bibr">80</xref>) (<xref rid="tab2" ref-type="table">Table 2</xref>). Also, various studies have shown that brucellosis has a significant influence on Middle Eastern countries, where contaminated milk products have been responsible for several outbreaks of human brucellosis during the previous two decades (<xref rid="ref75" ref-type="bibr">75</xref>, <xref rid="ref91" ref-type="bibr">91</xref>). The knowledge and awareness of farmers about brucellosis significantly reduce <italic>Brucella</italic> infection in animals (<xref rid="ref98" ref-type="bibr">98</xref>). The large-scale organized dairy farms have higher incidence rates than the individual native animals, which could be related to the disease&#x2019;s higher prevalence in exotic and cross-bred animals compared to native cattle (<xref rid="ref77" ref-type="bibr">77</xref>). Disease transmission usually occurs through natural mating/artificial insemination and physical contact and exposure to affected animals (<xref rid="ref99" ref-type="bibr">99</xref>). The microorganism is more vulnerable to infection in sexually mature pregnant cattle than in sexually immature cattle of either sex, and susceptibility increases as the pregnancy progresses (<xref rid="ref100" ref-type="bibr">100</xref>). Most animals get infected and harbor infection for the rest of their lives.</p>

              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="2" colspan="1">Human Factors</td>

              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">

              <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref95" ref-type="bibr">95</xref>)</td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">

</td>
              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">

</td>
              <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref58" ref-type="bibr">58</xref>)</td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="2" colspan="1">Agro-ecological factors</td>
              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Origin</p></list-item></list>
</td>
              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Sero-positivity differs in different parts of a country</p></list-item></list>
</td>
              <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref96" ref-type="bibr">96</xref>)</td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Presence of dogs, cats, and wildlife</p></list-item></list>
</td>
              <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Their presence is suspected as a source of infection</p></list-item></list>
</td>
              <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref97" ref-type="bibr">97</xref>)</td>
            </tr>
          </tbody>
        </table>
      </table-wrap>
      <p>The prevalence of disease and the difficulty of controlling infection in a population are closely related to herd size and animal density (<xref rid="ref101" ref-type="bibr">101</xref>). Animal species also affect disease susceptibility, and so a survey carried out for cattle, camels, sheep, and goats in a pastoral area in Isiolo County, Kenya, using RBPT and iELISA showed that camels and cattle have a greater incidence of brucellosis than sheep and goats (<xref rid="ref102" ref-type="bibr">102</xref>).</p>
      <p>According to the chairman of the Egyptian Chamber of Food Industries, social misconceptions about the nutritional quality of pasteurized milk have led to a decrease in the consumption of commercial milk products. More and more people buy fresh, unprocessed raw milk from small-scale dairy producers to account for up to 80% of the total milk industry (up to 4 billion liters per year) (<xref rid="ref103" ref-type="bibr">103</xref>). In Fayoum province, Upper Egypt, Abdel-Hamid and her group have investigated the risk factors and molecular genotyping of <italic>B. melitensis</italic> strains. From a total of 183 cattle cases, 78 (42.6%) were seropositive cattle cases. All seropositive cattle were female, and 85.9% (<italic>n</italic> =&#x2009;67) of them were older than 2&#x2009;years. One-third of the seropositive cattle (<italic>n</italic> =&#x2009;28) were reared alone. Nine of the seropositive cattle (11.5%) and one of the seronegative cattle (0.9%) had abortions. According to the socio-demographic variables, uneducated individuals, workers of an animal-related occupation, or owners of household cattle had a greater risk of brucellosis. In addition, in a multivariable model, soft cheese consumption was 2.3 times more likely to be responsible for brucellosis cases than controls (<italic>p</italic> 0.03) (<xref rid="ref104" ref-type="bibr">104</xref>).</p>
      <p>In France, the country has been declared free since 2005. So, a study was performed to measure the impact of veterinarians and all local institutional stakeholders on the compulsory notification of bovine abortions by farmers. The results showed that the proportion of notifying farmers was influenced by the number of veterinarians per practice and the veterinary practice&#x2019;s membership of a technical association (<xref rid="ref105" ref-type="bibr">105</xref>). In South China, the major source of brucellosis for human infection was goats, so a cross-sectional study was performed to investigate the true prevalence and risk factors for goat brucellosis in Ningxiang County, South China. The herd prevalence in commercial goat farms was 4.5% with nine potential risk factors identified by logistic regression analysis (<italic>p</italic> &lt;&#x2009;0.2). Among these factors, introduction in the past 12&#x2009;months, self-breeding, and safe disposal of sick or dead animals were the factors with the strongest association with disease presence (<xref rid="ref106" ref-type="bibr">106</xref>). In Nepal, another cross-sectional study was performed to investigate brucellosis in sheep and goats. At the farm level, 31.6% of sheep farms and 3.3% of goat farms were seropositive to brucellosis. Age&#x2009;&gt;&#x2009;1.5&#x2009;years (older sheep; <italic>p</italic> 0.02) and herd size &gt;100 (larger herds; <italic>p</italic> 0.03) were demonstrated as significant risk factors for brucellosis in the sheep population. While in the goat population, none of them was identified as a significant factor. Because sheep were frequently moved for grazing and selling, a management scheme for the sheep population should be implemented with strict biosecurity (<xref rid="ref107" ref-type="bibr">107</xref>).</p>
    </sec>
    <sec id="sec4">
      <label>4.</label>
      <title>Immunity</title>
      <sec id="sec5">
        <label>4.1.</label>
        <title>Innate immunity</title>
        <p>The first line of defense against pathogen attacks is the innate immune system. Anatomical barriers (skin and internal epithelial layers), secretory molecules (chemokines and cytokines, complement system, and opsonins), cellular populations such as phagocytes [neutrophils, monocytes/macrophages, and dendritic cells (DCs)], and innate lymphocyte subsets [natural killer (NK) cells and T cells] are all parts of the innate immune system (<xref rid="ref108" ref-type="bibr">108</xref>). <italic>Brucella</italic> antigenic components, such as lipopolysaccharide (LPS), Type IV secretion system (T4SS), BvrR/BvrS, outer membrane protein (Omp), proline racemase subunit A (PrpA), and Btp1, regulate the unique techniques that enable <italic>Brucella</italic> to elude the immune system. Intracellular survival, slowed phagocytosis, antibacterial action, and tumor necrosis factor-alpha (TNF-&#x3B1;) production are among these mechanisms (<xref rid="ref109" ref-type="bibr">109</xref>).</p>
        <p><italic>Brucella</italic> are considered resilient pathogens that elude innate immunity and stimulate polymorphonuclear cells (PMNCs) to enhance their microbicidal activity, allowing leukocytes to live longer and resist phagocyte mechanisms (<xref rid="ref110" ref-type="bibr">110</xref>). It was discovered that eliminating PMNCs before inducing adaptive immunity helped mice to get rid of bacteria, which in turn proved that neutrophils suppress the host immune response against <italic>Brucella</italic> (<xref rid="ref111" ref-type="bibr">111</xref>). The interaction of pattern recognition receptors (PRRs) and pathogen-associated molecular patterns (PAMPs) allows host cells to recognize threatening compounds and encourages the body to figure out how to fight pathogens (<xref rid="ref112" ref-type="bibr">112</xref>). <italic>Brucella</italic> LPS influences the complement system; thereby, the O-chain lacking a free hydroxyl is beneficial for connecting with C3, which suppresses the formation of C3a and C5a by associating with <italic>Brucella</italic> LPS&#x2019;s unique O-chain and avoiding host immunity (<xref rid="ref113" ref-type="bibr">113</xref>) (<xref rid="fig3" ref-type="fig">Figure 3</xref>). <italic>Brucella</italic> flagellin is also crucial for immune evasion and lacks the distinctive characteristic identified by toll-like receptor-5 (TLR5) (<xref rid="ref114" ref-type="bibr">114</xref>). In addition, host peroxiredoxin 6 (Prdx6), a bifunctional protein with peroxidase and phospholipase activities, has a function in <italic>Brucella</italic> infection by increasing the intracellular survival of the <italic>B. suis</italic> S2 strain (<xref rid="ref115" ref-type="bibr">115</xref>).</p>
        <fig position="float" id="fig3">
          <label>Figure 3</label>
          <caption>
            <p>LPS is a pivotal component of <italic>Brucella&#x2019;s</italic> pathogenicity and aids in the pathogen&#x2019;s ability to evade the host immune system. TLR4 is not recognized by <italic>Brucella</italic> LPS&#x2019;s acetyl side chain (C28), which prevents the host immune system from being monitored. The O-chain found in <italic>Brucella</italic> LPS can block complement C3 from producing C3a and C5a, which in turn can stop neutrophils from degranulation and releasing myeloperoxidase (MPO) and other lysosomal chemicals that could otherwise be collected by the host immune system.</p>
          </caption>
          <graphic xlink:href="fvets-10-1255239-g003" position="float"/>
        </fig>
      </sec>
      <sec id="sec6">
        <label>4.2.</label>
        <title>Intracellular life of <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens</title>
        <p>Intracellular pathogens are characterized by many occult pathways that need further experimental work to overcome the growing threat of many zoonotic pathogens (<xref rid="ref116" ref-type="bibr">116</xref>). In parallel, the growing concern regarding their resistance to different antimicrobials made further studying of their interaction with host cells increasingly important (<xref rid="ref117" ref-type="bibr">117&#x2013;119</xref>). <italic>Brucella</italic> has a multistage, complex intracellular replication process that affects the endocytic, secretory, and autophagic compartments through type IV secretion system (T4SS)-mediated delivery of bacterial effectors. These effectors enhance the conversion of the initial endosome-like <italic>Brucella</italic>-containing vacuole (eBCV) into a replication-permissive organelle derived from the host endoplasmic reticulum replicating BCV (rBCV), after that, to an autophagy-related vacuole (aBCV) that mediates bacterial egress (<xref rid="ref120" ref-type="bibr">120</xref>).</p>
        <p>T4SS regulates the inflammatory response and manipulates vesicle trafficking inside host cells, thereby playing crucial roles in the inhibition of the host immune response and intracellular survival during infection. In brief, after the entrance of <italic>Brucella</italic> into the host cell, it lives in acidified phagosomal compartments that are recognized as (eBCVs), and the pH can reach 4, which is essential for the survival of <italic>Brucella</italic> and the intracellular expression of the T4SS. The eBCVs avoid fusion with the terminally degraded lysosomes, thus ensuring not only the intracellular survival of bacteria but also triggering intracellular bacterial growth before rBCV is formed (<xref rid="ref121" ref-type="bibr">121</xref>). The rBCVs maintain <italic>Brucella&#x2019;s</italic> chronic intracellular persistence in the host. On the other hand, aBCVs are thought to be crucial for bacterial infection and cell-to-cell spread in the host and are created when the rBCVs engage with elements of the host cell&#x2019;s autophagy process (<xref rid="ref121" ref-type="bibr">121</xref>). Overall, <italic>Brucella</italic> can resist the phagocytic bactericidal effect; and it can also induce the host cells to create a microenvironment that is favorable for bacterial survival, reproduction, and replication. This ability allows <italic>Brucella</italic> to remain in the host cells for extended periods, which ultimately results in the development of chronic persistent infection (<xref rid="ref113" ref-type="bibr">113</xref>) (<xref rid="fig4" ref-type="fig">Figure 4</xref>).</p>
        <fig position="float" id="fig4">
          <label>Figure 4</label>
          <caption>
            <p>Mechanisms causing poor antigen presentation, intracellular trafficking, and penetration of <italic>Brucella</italic> inside macrophage: <italic>Brucella</italic> has developed a number of strategies to inhibit DCs&#x2019; and macrophages&#x2019; efficient antigen presentation, impairing the development of an adaptive immune system. (A) <italic>Brucella</italic> lipoprotein recognition by TLR2 results in IL-6-dependent suppression of the transcription factor CIITA, which reduces the transcription and expression of MHC-II that is triggered by IFN-&#x3B3;. Additionally, <italic>B. abortus</italic> (Ba) LPS reaches the cell surface where it joins with MHC-II molecules to generate macrodomains that prevent peptides from being presented to CD4+ T cells. Since <italic>B. abortus</italic> RNA detection results in the retention of MHCI molecules within the Golgi apparatus via TLR8 and the EGFR pathway, this impairment also affects cytotoxic CD8+ T cells. Pro-inflammatory cytokine production and the expression of co-stimulatory molecules in DCs are decreased because of the Omp25-SLAMF1 interaction&#x2019;s restriction of NF-k&#x3B2; translocation to the nucleus. The maturation of DCs is regulated by the <italic>Brucella</italic> effectors BtpA and BtpB, which are translocated to the cytoplasm during infection and disrupt TLR2 and TLR4 signaling. Due to the Ba LPS&#x2019;s unusual shape, particularly its core, it is poorly recognized by the TLR4-MD2 complex in both cell types, which prevents full activation, NF-k&#x3B2; translocation, and inhibits DCs maturation and T-cell activation. Red represents nuclear phosphorylated active NF-&#x3B2; dimers. The T4SS effectors, the LPS O-chain, OMP22, OMP25d, and cyclic 1, 2-d-glucan (C&#x3B2;G) assist in the remodeling of the lipid-rich regions of the exterior vacuole that results in a fusion with lysosomes for bacterial replication. T4SS proteins in the cytosol of the host cell enable interaction with the endoplasmic reticulum that is converted into the replicative vacuole. The black and orange circles characterize membrane vesicle trafficking from the endolysosomal pathway, Golgi apparatus, and endoplasmic reticulum to the <italic>Brucella</italic>-containing vacuoles (BCVs). The alteration in the colors of the BCV membranes symbolizes their change in structure as they switch from eBCVs to rBCVs. The outer blue membrane of the aBCV shows the engulfment of the rBCV by the host cell autophagosomal pathway.</p>
          </caption>
          <graphic xlink:href="fvets-10-1255239-g004" position="float"/>
        </fig>
      </sec>
      <sec id="sec7">
        <label>4.3.</label>
        <title>Acquired immunity</title>
        <p>The other arm of host defense is acquired immunity. It consists of T lymphocytes, which are involved in cytokine production and cytotoxicity (cellular immunity), in addition to antibody-producing B lymphocytes (humoral immunity) (<xref rid="ref108" ref-type="bibr">108</xref>). Primarily, the antigen-specific T cells secrete interferon-gamma (IFN-&#x3B3;) as part of the Th1 immune response against <italic>Brucella</italic>. Then, the IFN-&#x3B3; activates macrophage bactericidal machinery, enhances the production of antigen presenting cells (APCs), stimulates cytotoxic T-cells (CTL)-mediated cytotoxicity, and potentiates infected macrophage apoptosis (<xref rid="fig5" ref-type="fig">Figure 5</xref>). Finally, antibody-mediated opsonization (by IgG1, IgG2a, and IgG3) improves the phagocytic uptake of bacteria by lowering the initial level of <italic>Brucella</italic> infection (<xref rid="ref122" ref-type="bibr">122</xref>). Studies showed that <italic>Brucella</italic> induces APCs to generate IL-2 and activates NK cells. These NK cells release TNF-&#x3B1;, IFN-&#x3B3;, granulocyte-macrophage colony-stimulating factor (GM-CSF), and other cytokines that play a key role in Th1 and type 1 CD8+ T-cell (Tc1) responses (<xref rid="ref112" ref-type="bibr">112</xref>). TNF-&#x3B1; can boost macrophage bactericidal ability. On the other side, IL-12 can activate the Th1 immune response and produce IFN- &#x3B3; (<xref rid="ref123" ref-type="bibr">123</xref>). IFN- &#x3B3; secretion controls MHC-I and MHC-II expressions, which in turn, works for <italic>Brucella</italic> elimination through mediating Th1 immune response (<xref rid="ref111" ref-type="bibr">111</xref>, <xref rid="ref124" ref-type="bibr">124</xref>). All in all, the decreased CD8+ T-lymphocyte activation, IL-12, and TNF-&#x3B1; lead to immunosuppression, and promote <italic>Brucella</italic> multiplication and infection persistence (<xref rid="ref125" ref-type="bibr">125</xref>).</p>
        <fig position="float" id="fig5">
          <label>Figure 5</label>
          <caption>
            <p><italic>Brucella</italic> can stop APCs from secreting IL-2, which subsequently prevents the production of inflammatory substances such as IFN-&#x3B3; and TNF-&#x3B1; by NK cells. <italic>Brucella</italic> can block IFN-&#x3B3;-mediated phagocytosis to avoid being destroyed by the immune system. <italic>Brucella</italic> interferes with the Th1 immune response causing macrophages to lower IL-12 release and preventing DCs from activating T lymphocytes. It also impacts DCs maturation by blocking the TLR2 receptor pathway.</p>
          </caption>
          <graphic xlink:href="fvets-10-1255239-g005" position="float"/>
        </fig>
        <p>Recent studies clarified the roles of <italic>Brucella</italic> TIR protein 1 (Btp1), LPS, and PrpA as being significant immunomodulatory molecules with the capability to modify host immune mechanisms. Moreover, they can inhibit the secretion of IFN-&#x3B3; and increase the secretion of IL-10 affecting Th1 immune response (<xref rid="ref126" ref-type="bibr">126</xref>). Btp1 shows a sequence resembling the Toll/IL-1 receptors (TIRs) domain family. Different studies investigated the role of Btp1 in DCs maturation due to the significance of the TIR domain in TLR signaling. Btp1 inhibits both the production of proinflammatory cytokines and DCs maturation which leads to inhibition of TLR2 and TLR4 signaling. <italic>Brucella</italic> lumazine synthase (BLS) also induces negative effects by blocking the TLR4-myeloid differentiation protein-2 (MD2) complex and inducing CD8+ T-lymphocyte toxicity. This inhibition of CD8+ T-cell killing of <italic>Brucella</italic> target cells represents an adaptive immune evasion strategy (<xref rid="ref127" ref-type="bibr">127</xref>). In addition, Btp1 binds to adapter TIRAP (TIR domain-containing adaptor protein) at the cell membrane and blocks NF-&#x3BA;&#x3B2; activation (<xref rid="ref128" ref-type="bibr">128</xref>). PrpA also plays a significant role in the early stages of <italic>Brucella</italic> infection by regulating IFN-&#x3B3;, TNF-&#x3B1;, IL-10, and transforming growth factor-1 (TGF-1) pathways (<xref rid="ref129" ref-type="bibr">129</xref>) (<xref rid="fig6" ref-type="fig">Figure 6</xref>).</p>
        <fig position="float" id="fig6">
          <label>Figure 6</label>
          <caption>
            <p>Summary of immune escaping Strategies of <italic>Brucella</italic>: During the early stages of infection, TNF-&#x3B1; and IL-12 are secreted by DCs and macrophage cells as a result of the detection of <italic>Brucella</italic>, which promotes intracellular survival and replication. Flagellin suppresses the TLR5 receptor, whereas <italic>Brucella</italic> produces the Btp1/TcpB protein, which blocks the TLR2/4 signaling pathway. The <italic>Brucella</italic> LPS O-antigen binds to C3, preventing the complement cascade from being activated. <italic>Brucella</italic> blocks neutrophil degranulation and NK cell activation, interfering with the host&#x2019;s innate immune response. These bacteria produce Btp1/TcpB to prevent DCs maturation. <italic>Brucella</italic> LPS prevents antigen presentation and interferes with the host&#x2019;s innate and adaptive defense, making chronic infection more likely. TNF-&#x3B1; secretion is necessary for <italic>Brucella</italic> lipoprotein to promote T-cell apoptosis, which in turn directly suppresses the T-cell response. Additionally, <italic>Brucella</italic> prevents macrophage apoptosis and autophagolysosomal fusion, which represents the primary evading mechanisms of <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens. The end effect of interfering with these mechanisms is the development of human brucellosis and its clinical symptoms in natural hosts.</p>
          </caption>
          <graphic xlink:href="fvets-10-1255239-g006" position="float"/>
        </fig>
        <sec id="sec8">
          <label>4.3.1.</label>
          <title>Vaccination</title>
          <sec id="sec9">
            <label>4.3.1.1.</label>
            <title>Requirement of brucellosis vaccines</title>
            <p>Although <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccines have been employed as a control measure, they are not 100% successful in preventing the disease (<xref rid="ref130" ref-type="bibr">130</xref>). Average exposure to <italic>Brucella</italic> protects only 65% of vaccinated animals (<xref rid="ref131" ref-type="bibr">131</xref>). The requirements for an optimal <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccination have remained unchanged since they were first established many years ago. Such a vaccine should be safe and effective, does not enhance antibodies interfering with serodiagnosis, cannot be transmitted to humans or other animals (including no contamination of milk, edible organs, dairy products, or meat), be stable <italic>in vitro</italic> and <italic>in vivo</italic>, be easily cultivable for large-scale production, and be supplemented with markers to be differentiated from the field isolates (<xref rid="ref29" ref-type="bibr">29</xref>).</p>
          </sec>
          <sec id="sec10">
            <label>4.3.1.2.</label>
            <title>Classic attenuated <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccines</title>
            <p><italic>Brucella</italic> S19, RB51, 45/20, and Rev1 strains are commonly used for designing vaccines to protect livestock against <italic>Brucella</italic> infections and subsequent abortions (<xref rid="ref132" ref-type="bibr">132</xref>, <xref rid="ref133" ref-type="bibr">133</xref>) (<xref rid="tab3" ref-type="table">Table 3</xref>). Natural attenuation of the erythritol catabolic genes in <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19 resulted in the loss of a 720-bp area compared to the primary strain which resulted in a less virulent strain (<xref rid="ref137" ref-type="bibr">137</xref>). Vaccination with this attenuated strain stimulates a high level of immunity and protects animals against <italic>Brucella</italic> infection for a long period, which can extend to the entire productive life of the animal (<xref rid="ref138" ref-type="bibr">138</xref>). On the other hand, the S19 vaccine has several drawbacks, including interfering with serodiagnostic brucellosis tests, inducing abortions in some vaccinated and pregnant animals, reducing milk production, and being pathogenic in humans (<xref rid="ref139" ref-type="bibr">139</xref>). It is administered to female calves between the ages of 3 and 6&#x2009;months in a single subcutaneous dosage of (2.5&#x2013;12&#x2009;&#xD7;&#x2009;10<sup>10</sup>) colony-forming units (CFUs) (<xref rid="ref140" ref-type="bibr">140</xref>). Adult cattle can be injected with a lower dosage of organisms subcutaneously, but some animals will acquire persisting antibody titers and may abort and excrete the vaccine strain in their milk. When used for calves aged 3&#x2013;5&#x2009;months, the conjunctival technique is completely safe and minimizes the risk of serological interference. It can also be given to cattle of any age as one or two doses of 5&#x2009;&#xD7;&#x2009;10<sup>9</sup> CFU via the conjunctival route, which protects without a prolonged antibody response and minimizes the risk of abortion and microbial shedding in milk when vaccinating adult cattle (<xref rid="ref141" ref-type="bibr">141</xref>). Subsequently, Saidu et al. confirmed that the intraocular route is still the safest route for vaccinating adult cattle than the subcutaneous route (<xref rid="ref134" ref-type="bibr">134</xref>).</p>
            <table-wrap position="float" id="tab3">
              <label>Table 3</label>
              <caption>
                <p>The advantages and disadvantages of the classic attenuated <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccines.</p>
              </caption>
              <table frame="hsides" rules="groups">
                <thead>
                  <tr>
                    <th align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Vaccines</th>
                    <th align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Advantages</th>
                    <th align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Disadvantages</th>
                    <th align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Notes</th>
                    <th align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">References</th>
                  </tr>
                </thead>
                <tbody>
                  <tr>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Smooth <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Efficacy in control/eradication programs has been demonstrated (USA, EU countries, and Australia).</p></list-item><list-item><p>It provides superior protection for cattle against <italic>B. abortus</italic> and <italic>B. melitensis</italic></p></list-item><list-item><p>A single dose provides effective protection for the rest of life.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">

</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">

</td>
                    <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">

</td>
                  </tr>
                  <tr>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic>B. melitensis</italic> Rev. 1</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Efficacy in control/eradication programs has been demonstrated (France, Italy).</p></list-item><list-item><p>Both <italic>B. melitensis</italic> and <italic>B. ovis</italic> are susceptible to it.</p></list-item><list-item><p>Males and young replacements are safe.</p></list-item><list-item><p>A single dose provides effective protection for the rest of life.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Abortion is at a high level.</p></list-item><list-item><p>Serological interference with RBT, CFT, indirect and competitive ELISA, fluorescence polarization assay, and other S-LPS tests.</p></list-item><list-item><p>It exhibits substantial human virulence, and it is resistant to streptomycin.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>By avoiding the vaccination of pregnant animals <italic>via</italic> the conjunctival route, safety concerns are reduced.</p></list-item><list-item><p>When given to young animals <italic>via</italic> the conjunctival route, serological interference is reduced.</p></list-item><list-item><p>Standard serological tests can be used to diagnose human infections; however, treatment strategies that do not use streptomycin are required.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                    <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="simple"><list-item><p>(<xref rid="ref135" ref-type="bibr">135</xref>)</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                  </tr>
                  <tr>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Rough <italic>B. abortus</italic> RB51</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>When young animals are given lower doses, serological interference is reduced (particularly by the conjunctival route).</p></list-item><list-item><p>Conventional serological testing can be used to diagnose human infections, and standard antibiotic treatment can be used to treat them.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>It causes fewer abortions in cattle compared to the S19 vaccine.</p></list-item><list-item><p>Inducing protective immunity is less successful than S19 (efficacy or revaccination unknown)</p></list-item><list-item><p>The protection duration is unknown.</p></list-item><list-item><p>Cattle protection against <italic>B. melitensis</italic> is unclear.</p></list-item><list-item><p>Indirect and competitive ELISAs, as well as fluorescence polarization assays, are all affected by serological interference.</p></list-item><list-item><p>It is resistant to rifampicin.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                    <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>It is currently licensed in non-pregnant female cattle (4&#x2013;12) months.</p></list-item><list-item><p>There is no evidence of eradication efficacy. There are no adequate serological diagnostic tests for human infections, and therapy needs non-rifampicin regimens.</p></list-item><list-item><p>It has been documented to cause human disease, most commonly through occupational exposures such as needle sticks.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                    <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="simple"><list-item><p>(<xref rid="ref29" ref-type="bibr">29</xref>, <xref rid="ref136" ref-type="bibr">136</xref>)</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                  </tr>
                </tbody>
              </table>
            </table-wrap>
            <p><italic>B. abortus</italic> strain RB51, a rifampicin-resistant mutant, was isolated as a single rough colony from a virulent smooth strain of <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308. Serial subculturing of <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308 resulted in the deletion of the wbo A gene, which encodes a glycosyltransferase required for O-side chain production (<xref rid="ref29" ref-type="bibr">29</xref>, <xref rid="ref142" ref-type="bibr">142</xref>). Unlike the strain 19 vaccine, the strain RB51 vaccine does not affect <italic>Brucella</italic> serodiagnosis results (<xref rid="ref143" ref-type="bibr">143</xref>). Therefore, it has replaced the <italic>B. abortus</italic> strain 19 vaccine, which is well-known as the calf hood vaccine in many countries. The term calfhood vaccine means vaccination of heifers between 4 and 10&#x2009;months of age with <italic>Brucella abortus</italic> strain 19, and the best age of vaccination is at 5&#x2009;months (<xref rid="ref144" ref-type="bibr">144</xref>). RB51 vaccine can infect humans, and one of its primary drawbacks is the resistance to rifampicin antibiotic, which is used for human brucellosis therapy (<xref rid="ref145" ref-type="bibr">145</xref>). <italic>B. melitensis</italic> Rev1 vaccine could be used to protect sheep and goats against brucellosis (<xref rid="ref146" ref-type="bibr">146</xref>). However, it has a negative effect because it might cause abortions in pregnant animals after vaccination (<xref rid="ref24" ref-type="bibr">24</xref>). Aside from being virulent for humans, lactating female animals can shed the vaccinal strain through milk, which can infect other animals and interfere with serological diagnosis (<xref rid="ref147" ref-type="bibr">147</xref>).</p>
            <p><italic>B. abortus</italic> 45/20 strain was created by passing the <italic>B. abortus</italic> 45/0 virulent strain 20 times in guinea pigs, although multiple studies have shown that it can revert to smooth virulent forms when used as a live vaccine. So far, the 45/20 vaccine is combined with an oil-based adjuvant, needing repeat vaccination (<xref rid="ref143" ref-type="bibr">143</xref>). A systematic review and meta-analysis were performed to recalculate the vaccine efficacy of <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19 and RB51 vaccine strains suggesting that a dose of 10<sup>9</sup> CFU for S19 and 10<sup>10</sup> CFU for RB51 are the most effective for the prevention of abortion and infection caused by <italic>B. abortus</italic> (<xref rid="ref136" ref-type="bibr">136</xref>).</p>
            <p>The DIVA vaccines (Differentiating Infected from Vaccinated Animals) are defined as the vaccines that distinguish infected and vaccinated animals. S19 and Rev1 vaccine problems can be solved using DIVA vaccines. There are two main strategies to produce these vaccines. First, the removal of the diagnostic antigens found in field strains from current vaccines. In other words, creating <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccine candidates free from O-chain and NH-polysaccharides or synthesis of immunogenic proteins using <italic>Brucella</italic> mutants that could be used as neutral antigenic markers. Second, the addition of the foreign (xenogenic) antigens to the classical live-attenuated <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccine strains, thereby enabling the development of diagnostic tests that can recognize vaccinated animals in infected environments (<xref rid="ref141" ref-type="bibr">141</xref>). Meanwhile, DIVA vaccines that can replace S19 and Rev1 vaccines should protect at least as good as the primary vaccines (<xref rid="ref29" ref-type="bibr">29</xref>). Many studies have suggested several vaccine candidates for DIVA vaccines such as VjbR and BtpA proteins contributed to the virulence of the Y3 and M5 <italic>Brucella</italic> strains. Zur protein, ABC transporters, and thiamine metabolism-associated proteins may play crucial roles in <italic>Brucella</italic> survival and pathogenesis (<xref rid="ref148" ref-type="bibr">148</xref>). In parallel, Uslu and Erganis revealed that <italic>B. melitensis</italic> Rev.1 &#x394;Omp19 can act as a DIVA marker vaccine using an ELISA test for the detection of the Omp19 protein (<xref rid="ref149" ref-type="bibr">149</xref>).</p>
          </sec>
          <sec id="sec11">
            <label>4.3.1.3.</label>
            <title>Genomics and proteomics approaches for improved <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccines</title>
            <sec id="sec12">
              <label>4.3.1.3.1.</label>
              <title><italic>Brucella</italic> subunit vaccines</title>
              <p>Several <italic>Brucella</italic> fragments, such as recombinant peptides, proteins, DNA, LPS, and outer membrane proteins (OMPs) are now being tested as subunit vaccines against <italic>B. abortus</italic> (<xref rid="ref147" ref-type="bibr">147</xref>). They have been proved to be a promising field for study and development because of their pioneer advantages over traditional live-attenuated vaccines, including high safety with no residual virulence and the ability to be used in people and pregnant animals. However, although they provide appealing alternatives to traditional live-attenuated vaccines, they face significant challenges. The low protective efficacy and the requirement for adjuvant and booster doses are among them. Consequently, subunit vaccines can be improved by using potent T-cell antigens that produce a Th1 immune response as dominant immunity against brucellosis (<xref rid="ref150" ref-type="bibr">150</xref>, <xref rid="ref151" ref-type="bibr">151</xref>) (<xref rid="tab4" ref-type="table">Table 4</xref>).</p>
              <table-wrap position="float" id="tab4">
                <label>Table 4</label>
                <caption>
                  <p>The immune response of <italic>Brucella</italic> subunit vaccine candidates.</p>
                </caption>
                <table frame="hsides" rules="groups">
                  <thead>
                    <tr>
                      <th align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Subunit vaccine candidates</th>
                      <th align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Immune response</th>
                      <th align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">References</th>
                    </tr>
                  </thead>

                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">

</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref158" ref-type="bibr">158</xref>, <xref rid="ref159" ref-type="bibr">159</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic>Brucella</italic> OMP25c recombinant protein combined with Freund&#x2019;s adjuvant produced</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Produces both Th1 and Th2 immune responses in mice, with protection levels comparable to the S19 strain.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref160" ref-type="bibr">160</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">AspC, Dps, Ndk, and lnpB as <italic>B. abortus</italic> proteins subunit vaccines</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Elicits high levels of IgG2a titer and exhibited equal protective efficacy to the RB51 strain.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref161" ref-type="bibr">161</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">3E-IL2 <italic>Brucella</italic> recombinant vaccines</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Stimulate the immune system to create Th1 cytokines and antibodies.</p></list-item><list-item><p>Generate larger amounts of IFN-&#x3B3; and IL-2.</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref162" ref-type="bibr">162</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">DNA vaccines encoding BAB1-0263 and BAB1-0278 from ORFs of GI-3</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<list list-type="bullet"><list-item><p>Stimulate both cellular and humeral immunity with a high level of IF-&#x3B3;.</p></list-item><list-item><p>DNA vaccines express BAB1-0278 protected mice when challenged with <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308 strain</p></list-item></list>
</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref137" ref-type="bibr">137</xref>)<break/>(<xref rid="ref144" ref-type="bibr">144</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                  </tbody>
                </table>
              </table-wrap>
              <p>OMP16, OMP19, liposomes protein L7/L12, OMP25, p39 (a potential periplasmic binding protein), and AsnC are important <italic>Brucella</italic> subunit proteins (<xref rid="ref152" ref-type="bibr">152&#x2013;154</xref>). In general, these antigens stimulate Th1 immunity and provide protection similar to that provided by the commercial S19 live vaccine (<xref rid="ref153" ref-type="bibr">153</xref>, <xref rid="ref158" ref-type="bibr">158</xref>). Other <italic>Brucella</italic> subunit vaccines such as dihydrolipoamide succinyltransferase (rE2o) and cysteine synthase A (rCysK) are capable of inducing Th2 immunity with a relatively low protection level (<xref rid="ref155" ref-type="bibr">155</xref>, <xref rid="ref156" ref-type="bibr">156</xref>). In addition, compared to the S19 vaccine, the SurA and DnaK cytosolic proteins elicit lower levels of protection in a mouse model (<xref rid="ref157" ref-type="bibr">157</xref>). Additionally, <italic>B. abortus</italic> rAdk and rSecB have been suggested as promising subunit vaccine candidates. BP26 and BLS proteins are also among the top candidates for serological diagnosis of brucellosis (<xref rid="ref154" ref-type="bibr">154</xref>, <xref rid="ref163" ref-type="bibr">163</xref>).</p>
              <p>Vaccinating mice with a recombinant protein cocktail (rOMP19&#x2009;+&#x2009;rp39) resulted in Th1-mediated isotype antibodies and cellular immunity, which protected animals against the <italic>B. abortus</italic> 544 strain (<xref rid="ref158" ref-type="bibr">158</xref>). Afterward, the <italic>B. abortus</italic> chimeric subunit protein with OMP19 and p39 domains was used as a booster dose-induced Th1-type immune response (<xref rid="ref159" ref-type="bibr">159</xref>). Intriguingly, using <italic>Brucella</italic> OMP25c recombinant protein combined with Freund&#x2019;s adjuvant produced both Th1 and Th2 immune responses in mice, with protection levels similar to the S19 vaccine (<xref rid="ref160" ref-type="bibr">160</xref>). More interestingly, using a mixture of various recombinant <italic>B. abortus</italic> proteins, including AspC, Dps, Ndk, and lnpB as subunit vaccines, induced high levels of IgG2a titer and exhibited equal protective efficacy to the RB51 vaccine strain (<xref rid="ref161" ref-type="bibr">161</xref>). In comparison with other recombinant subunit proteins, 3E-IL2 inoculation could be a superior candidate for further study into the manufacture of <italic>Brucella</italic> recombinant vaccines (<xref rid="ref162" ref-type="bibr">162</xref>).</p>
            </sec>
            <sec id="sec13">
              <label>4.3.1.3.2.</label>
              <title>DNA vaccines</title>
              <p>Another type of subunit vaccine that induces strong humoral and cellular immune responses with repeated doses is the DNA-based <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccine (<xref rid="ref164" ref-type="bibr">164</xref>). Using laboratory and clinical testing, they were proven to be safe and effective. To examine their immunogenicity, BALB/c mice were immunized with multivalent DNA vaccines. These vaccines significantly induced the humoral immune response (IgM, IgG, and IgG2a) and cell-mediated immunity (high IFN-&#x3B3; and increased splenic lymphoproliferative response) (<xref rid="ref165" ref-type="bibr">165</xref>). The genomic island 3 (GI-3) region of <italic>B. abortus</italic> encodes multiple open reading frames (ORFs) that express critical antigens for microbial intracellular survival and pathogenicity. Therefore, the <italic>Brucella</italic> DNA vaccine based on the GI-3 region could be a good candidate for protection against <italic>B. abortus</italic> infection (<xref rid="ref166" ref-type="bibr">166</xref>). More specifically, DNA vaccines expressing BAB1-0263 or BAB1-0278 genes from GI-3 ORFs induced substantial levels of IFN-&#x3B3; production, which in turn elicited strong humoral and cellular immunity. In addition, when mice were challenged with the <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308 strain, a DNA vaccine expressing BAB1-0278 provided good protection (<xref rid="ref167" ref-type="bibr">167</xref>).</p>
              <p>In comparison with <italic>B. abortus</italic> RB51 vaccination, DNA vaccine encoding Cu-Zn superoxide dismutase (SOD)-IL-2 fusion protein stimulated IgG2a and TNF-&#x3B1; in mice, resulting in excellent protection against <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308 strain (<xref rid="ref165" ref-type="bibr">165</xref>). The combination of SOD with L7/L12 and BCSP31 generated a robust cytotoxic CD8+ T cell and specific IgG, resulting in a higher level of protection compared to <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19 (<xref rid="ref168" ref-type="bibr">168</xref>). In another investigation, <italic>Brucella</italic> genes (SOD, BCSP31, and L7/L12) were joined with several genes from <italic>Mycobacterium bovis</italic> or <italic>Mycobacterium TB,</italic> resulting in a dual efficient DNA vaccine for both infections (<italic>Brucella and Mycobacteria</italic>) (<xref rid="ref169" ref-type="bibr">169</xref>, <xref rid="ref170" ref-type="bibr">170</xref>). Another study showed that a divalent DNA vaccine expressing both <italic>B. abortus</italic> L7/L12 and OMP16 genes boosted robust cellular and humoral immunity in mice by inducing IFN-&#x3B3; and IgG2a production. In addition, compared to the univalent OMP16 or L7/L12 DNA vaccine, this divalent DNA vaccine induced higher levels of protection, even though the protective efficacy of the divalent OMP16 and L7/L12 was lower than that of the traditional RB51 vaccine (<xref rid="ref171" ref-type="bibr">171</xref>).</p>
              <p>The bacterial ghost (BG) vaccine is a novel strategy with great promise. In China, the <italic>B. abortus</italic> A19 strain is widely used as a vaccine. However, persistent pathogenicity in animals and humans is a common disadvantage (<xref rid="ref172" ref-type="bibr">172</xref>). In China, He et al. developed the <italic>Brucella abortus</italic> A19 bacterial ghost (A19BG) vaccine; it was inactivated using a two-step process that included biological lysis and hydrogen peroxide treatment, which, in turn, completely inactivated the viable bacterial cells found even at high concentration of 10<sup>10</sup> CFU/mL. Furthermore, no adverse effects have been noticed in guinea pigs. The levels of antibodies, interleukin-4, interferon-&#x3B3;, and CD4+ T cells in guinea pigs inoculated with the A19BG vaccine were comparable to those inoculated with the conventional A19 vaccine. In guinea pigs and cattle, vaccination with both A19BG and A19 vaccines provided equal protection against <italic>B. melitensis</italic> M28 (<xref rid="ref173" ref-type="bibr">173</xref>).</p>
            </sec>
            <sec id="sec14">
              <label>4.3.1.3.3.</label>
              <title>Genetically engineered live-attenuated vaccines</title>
              <p>The identification of genes linked to virulence or organism survival can help in creating safe and efficient novel vaccines. In comparison with traditional live-attenuated vaccines, currently developed live-attenuated vaccines can induce significant levels of protection (<xref rid="ref137" ref-type="bibr">137</xref>). The purine biosynthesis pathway genes, T4SS, virB genes, lipid A fatty acid transporting gene, ferrochelatase hem H mutant, phosphoglycerate kinase encoding gene, and the LPS biosynthesis pathway gene are considered under vaccinal development depending on multiple deletions in <italic>B. abortus</italic> genome that lead to significant attenuation (<xref rid="ref174" ref-type="bibr">174</xref>, <xref rid="ref175" ref-type="bibr">175</xref>). These mutations provide supreme protection compared to traditional live-attenuated vaccines. In mouse and human cell experiments, deletion of <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308 norD and high-affinity zinc uptake system (znuA) genes causes adequate attenuation. Furthermore, this live recombinant strain effectively enhances T cells and produces potent immunity against virulent challenges, compared to traditional RB51 vaccinated groups (<xref rid="ref176" ref-type="bibr">176</xref>).</p>
              <p>By deleting the phosphoglucomutase (pgm) gene of <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308, Ugalde et al. created a recombinant strain with no serodiagnostic interference and protective Th1 immune responses compared to the S19 strain (<xref rid="ref177" ref-type="bibr">177</xref>). Additionally, GntR, a transcriptional regulator of many virulent antigens of <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308, has been deleted resulting in an attenuated mutant with high protection levels in mice against the parental <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308 challenge (<xref rid="ref178" ref-type="bibr">178</xref>). In parallel, the deletion of NodV and NodW genes in <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308 resulted in an attenuated live vaccine with reduced survival in cell lines and mouse models, and it did not affect serological diagnosis (<xref rid="ref179" ref-type="bibr">179</xref>). In 2010, Yang et al. successfully deleted znuA and purE genes of <italic>B. abortus</italic> resulting in a live-attenuated mutant that required two doses to generate an appropriate immune response in mice (<xref rid="ref180" ref-type="bibr">180</xref>).</p>
              <p>The deletion of the cgs gene in <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19 increased the attenuation of the S19 strain without altering its protective efficacy against <italic>B. abortus</italic> 2,308 (<xref rid="ref181" ref-type="bibr">181</xref>). In addition, the deletion of the vjbR gene, which is responsible for intracellular <italic>Brucella</italic> persistence, resulted in a recombinant mutant with high levels of protection and reduced inflammation (<xref rid="ref182" ref-type="bibr">182</xref>). <italic>B. abortus</italic> targeted mutants with deletions in the membrane fusogenic protein (Mfp) or OMP19 genes decrease <italic>Brucella</italic> persistence in animal studies. However, challenge tests have revealed that traditional attenuated vaccines such as S19 and RB51 strains provide similar levels of protection (<xref rid="ref183" ref-type="bibr">183</xref>). The deletion of the wbkC gene of <italic>B. abortus</italic> (&#x394;wbkC), which translated to formyltransferase enzyme, showed a protection level similar to <italic>B. abortus</italic> rough strain RB51 avoiding the rifampicin resistance which is the main disadvantage of RB51. However, <italic>B. abortus</italic> &#x394;wbkC did not produce the same level of protectin when compared to <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19 and avoided interfering with the serological diagnosis which is also the main disadvantage of the S19 vaccine strain (<xref rid="ref184" ref-type="bibr">184</xref>). The Glycosyltransferase Wad C gene is involved in the production of the core oligosaccharide region of <italic>B. abortus</italic> LPS and is essential for efficient innate immune recognition (<xref rid="ref185" ref-type="bibr">185</xref>). The deletion of this gene produced a better immune response compared to those provided by the S19 strain (<xref rid="ref186" ref-type="bibr">186</xref>).</p>
              <p>Single and double gene deletions of the virulent <italic>B. abortus</italic> RB51 cydD and cydC genes have created significantly attenuated mutants (RB51&#x394;cydC, RB51&#x394;cydD, RB51&#x394;purD, RB51&#x394;cydC&#x394;cydD, and RB51&#x394;cydC&#x394;purD). Vaccination with a single dose of these mutants induced lower protective immunity in mice compared to the RB51 vaccine. However, a booster dose provided significant levels of protection in mice against the challenge of the virulent <italic>B. abortus</italic> strain 2,308 and <italic>B. canis</italic> strain 26 (<xref rid="ref145" ref-type="bibr">145</xref>, <xref rid="ref187" ref-type="bibr">187</xref>). Additionally, in South Korea, the double deletion in a field isolate of <italic>B. abortus</italic> biovar 1 (BA15) produced similar protective effects without the need for a subsequent booster shot (<xref rid="ref188" ref-type="bibr">188</xref>). In <italic>B. abortus</italic> biovar 1 strain IVKB9007, deletion of the ATP/GDP-binding protein motif A (p-loop) and the ATP-binding/permease protein resulted in attenuated mutants that were unable to replicate intracellularly in cell line models. These mutants induced significant levels of protection against the pathogenic <italic>B. abortus</italic> strain 544 (<xref rid="ref189" ref-type="bibr">189</xref>). Another study compared the whole-genome sequences of <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19 and A19 vaccine strains. Both strains were proved to have a significant degree of genetic similarity. The differences in genomic structure revealed that S19 had a 697&#x2009;bp deletion and loss of function on both eryC and eryD genes compared to the A19 strain (<xref rid="ref190" ref-type="bibr">190</xref>). Moreover, the Wzm/Wzt system has an important role in exporting the O-polysaccharide (O-PS) as the main virulence factor of <italic>Brucella</italic>. Single and double deletion mutants of wzm/wzt have been designed using the attenuated strain <italic>B. melitensis</italic> Rev1. Results revealed that Rev1&#x394;wzm mutant is an immunogenic and effective vaccine candidate against <italic>B. melitensis</italic> and <italic>B. ovis</italic> in mice even though it had low persistence. So, it has minimal serological interference in sheep making Rev1&#x394;wzm a highly promising vaccine candidate. Rev1&#x394;wzm is a highly promising vaccine candidate in sheep because it has minimal serological interference and is safe to be used in pregnant ewes (<xref rid="ref191" ref-type="bibr">191</xref>) (<xref rid="tab5" ref-type="table">Table 5</xref>).</p>
