                </table>
              </table-wrap>
            </sec>
            <sec id="sec15">
              <label>4.3.1.3.4.</label>
              <title>Vector-based <italic>B. abortus</italic> vaccines</title>
              <p>Several <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccines based on bacterial or viral vectors have recently been designed, and they provide an efficient method of delivering a range of heterologous or homologous antigens (<xref rid="ref192" ref-type="bibr">192</xref>) (<xref rid="tab6" ref-type="table">Table 6</xref>). The optimum method for stimulating the target host immune system against <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens can be achieved through induced cell-mediated immunity because of the intracellular tropism of this microorganism. These live vaccines produce many copies of the <italic>Brucella</italic> antigens because they reproduce inside the host cells. Despite all these benefits being highly appealing, there is no current efficient vector-based <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccine that provides the best protection.</p>
              <table-wrap position="float" id="tab6">
                <label>Table 6</label>
                <caption>
                  <p>The vector-based <italic>B. abortus</italic> vaccines.</p>
                </caption>
                <table frame="hsides" rules="groups">
                  <thead>
                    <tr>
                      <th align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Vector-based <italic>B. abortus</italic> vaccines</th>
                      <th align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Significance</th>
                      <th align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">References</th>
                    </tr>
                  </thead>
                  <tbody>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">A combination of <italic>Salmonella</italic> vectors expressing BCSP31, SOD, and OMP3b</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Increased Th1 response and enhanced protection</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref193" ref-type="bibr">193</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic>Salmonella</italic> as a vector that expressed ribosomal protein L7/L12 and the lumazine synthase enzyme</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Induced a Th1-type response but did not protect mice against <italic>B. abortus</italic> challenge.</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref194" ref-type="bibr">194</xref>, <xref rid="ref195" ref-type="bibr">195</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Attenuated <italic>Salmonella</italic> vector-based vaccine that expressed heterologous <italic>Brucella</italic> antigens (SOD, BLS, PrpA, and OMP19)</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Produced significant levels of IFN-&#x3B3; and provided adequate protective efficacy that was alternative to the traditional RB51 vaccine.</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref196" ref-type="bibr">196</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Influenza viral vector-<italic>B. abortus</italic> vaccine</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Provided equal protection to the <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19 vaccine.</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref197" ref-type="bibr">197</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                    <tr>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Adenovirus vector-based vaccine that expressed the p39 and lumazine synthase proteins of <italic>B. abortus</italic> for immunization of mice</td>
                      <td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Induced substantial humoral and cellular immune responses in a mouse model.</td>
                      <td align="center" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">(<xref rid="ref198" ref-type="bibr">198</xref>)</td>
                    </tr>
                  </tbody>
                </table>
              </table-wrap>
              <p>Many studies demonstrated that attenuated Salmonella strains can express various <italic>Brucella</italic> antigens, such as BLS, PrpA, Omp19, and Cu-Zn SOD, which were employed as vaccine vectors. These formulations with pure <italic>Brucella</italic> LPS were inoculated intraperitoneally in specific pathogen-free (SPF) mice and produced an efficient delivery with a significant (<italic>p</italic> &#x2264;&#x2009;0.05) reduction in splenic wild-type <italic>B. abortus</italic> 544 colonization compared to the control mice. More importantly, this vaccination system can provide a platform for the development of cross-protecting vaccines able to prevent multi-species brucellosis (<xref rid="ref199" ref-type="bibr">199</xref>).</p>
              <p>As vector-based vaccines, influenza viruses expressing <italic>Brucella</italic> ribosomal proteins L7/L12 and OMP16 have been produced (<xref rid="ref200" ref-type="bibr">200</xref>). Cross-protection against <italic>B. melitensis</italic> can be obtained using the <italic>B. abortus</italic> vaccine. First, pregnant heifers received an influenza viral vector <italic>B. abortus</italic> vaccine and have been protected from abortions. After challenging with <italic>B. melitensis</italic>16 M, a remarkable cross-protection (90&#x2013;100%) was obtained for the heifers, their calves, and fetuses. On the other hand, when compared to the <italic>B. abortus</italic> S19 vaccine, the influenza viral vector-<italic>B. abortus</italic> vaccine provided equal protection (<xref rid="ref197" ref-type="bibr">197</xref>). In terms of safety, this recombinant vector vaccine showed superior safety characters. Notably, this vaccine prime-booster immunization produced humoral and cellular immunity with long-term protection against <italic>B. abortus</italic> infection, particularly in pregnant heifers (<xref rid="ref201" ref-type="bibr">201</xref>, <xref rid="ref202" ref-type="bibr">202</xref>). In addition, adding OMP19 and SOD proteins to the vaccine formulation, as well as using montanide gel as an adjuvant, resulted in excellent protection in sheep and goats when challenged with <italic>B. melitensis</italic> (<xref rid="ref203" ref-type="bibr">203</xref>).</p>
              <p>An adenovirus vector-based vaccine was developed for protection against brucellosis and expressed the p39 and BLS proteins of <italic>B. abortus</italic>. This vaccine successfully induced strong humoral and cellular immune responses in a mouse model (<xref rid="ref198" ref-type="bibr">198</xref>).</p>
            </sec>
            <sec id="sec16">
              <label>4.3.1.3.5.</label>
              <title>Proteomics approaches</title>
              <p>Proteomics, the key post-genomic technique for analyzing the effects of regulatory mechanisms on the protein composition of microorganisms, has the potential to deal with issues of One-Health concern. It is a useful tool for researching gene expression, microbial physiology, and host&#x2013;pathogen interactions. Scientists have been using proteomics technology to decipher numerous mysterious areas of <italic>Brucella</italic> research since the start of the 21st century. Proteomics techniques, including sodium dodecyl sulfate-polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis (SDS-PAGE), two-dimensional gel electrophoresis (2DE), matrix-assisted laser desorption ionization&#x2013;time of flight mass spectrometry (MALDI-TOF MS), and label-free analysis, are advanced approaches for accurate analysis of pathogenic protein spots (<xref rid="ref204" ref-type="bibr">204</xref>).</p>
              <p>A study was performed to identify the phenotypic and pathophysiological variations among <italic>Brucella</italic> species (<italic>B. melitensis</italic> strain 16&#x2009;M and <italic>B. ovis</italic> REO198) and to correlate these variations with virulence elements. SDS-PAGE was used to separate protein extracts from the two <italic>Brucella</italic> species. The nano-scale liquid chromatographic tandem mass spectrometry (nLC&#x2013;MS/MS) was used to investigate the areas that were qualitatively and quantitatively different. A total of 274 and 606 proteins were identified for <italic>B. melitensis</italic> and <italic>B. ovis</italic>, respectively. Bioinformatics study was performed to examine their structural and functional characteristics. Outer membrane immunogenic protein N8LTS7 and outer membrane immunogenic protein Q45321 were identified as promising candidates for improving <italic>Brucella</italic> diagnostic procedures and vaccinations for <italic>B. ovis</italic> and <italic>B. melitensis</italic>, respectively (<xref rid="ref205" ref-type="bibr">205</xref>). Moreover, outer membrane vesicles (OMVs) have been suggested as a paradigm for the development of cellular vaccines because they contain immunogenic proteins. To characterize the pan-proteome of these vesicles, OMVs of <italic>B. suis</italic>, <italic>B. ovis</italic>, <italic>B. canis</italic>, and <italic>B. neotomae</italic> were isolated and analyzed using SDS-PAGE, transmission electron microscopy, and liquid chromatography-mass spectrometry (LC&#x2013;MS). Western blot was also used to identify antigenic proteins. Several homologous immunogenic proteins, including Omp25, Omp16, Omp2a, Omp31, SodC, and BhuA, were detected during <italic>in silico</italic> investigations. Anti-<italic>Brucella</italic> sera were able to identify the proteins in the vesicles from several <italic>Brucella</italic> species (<xref rid="ref206" ref-type="bibr">206</xref>).</p>
              <p>A comparative study between the vaccinal strain (<italic>B. melitensis</italic> Rev.1) and the field strain (<italic>B. melitensis</italic> 16&#x2009;M) enabled us to get the detailed <italic>Brucella</italic> protein repertoires using label-free shotgun proteomics investigation which would help in discriminating the vaccinated from infected animals (<xref rid="ref207" ref-type="bibr">207</xref>). MALDI-TOF MS, a quick, affordable, and trustworthy way for routinely identifying Brucellae, is an advanced technique used to reduce the time needed to identify pathogens and may soon replace the current conventional and molecular procedures. Hamidi et al. performed the MALDI-TOF MS technique for fast and trusty differentiation of <italic>B. abortus</italic> and <italic>B. melitensis</italic>, depending on proteomic mass patterns. They reported significant protein mass signals, which were mapped to ribosomal proteins and structural proteins, such as integration host factor subunit alpha, GNAT family N-acetyltransferase, HU family DNA-binding protein, cold-shock proteins, and ATP synthase subunit C. They used these specific biomarker peaks that have been recognized for virulent and vaccine strains as differentiating tools (<xref rid="ref208" ref-type="bibr">208</xref>). Furthermore, a unique MALDI-TOF MS reference library was created by Christoforidou et al., and the effectiveness of its performance on species-level identification was assessed using 75 <italic>Brucella</italic> spp. isolates. Analysis of mass peak profiles allowed for 100% accurate genus and species identification of <italic>Brucella</italic>. Despite the significant intrageneric similarity, the MALDI-TOF MS database was able to classify 47 out of 62 <italic>B. melitensis</italic> bv. 3 isolates at the biovar level (75.81%) (<xref rid="ref209" ref-type="bibr">209</xref>).</p>
              <p>Proteins and genes are differently expressed in <italic>B. abortus</italic> cultured under biofilm and planktonic conditions. 2DE and MALDI-TOF MS were used to separate and identify these proteins, respectively. 2DE revealed 20 protein spots that were differentially expressed between biofilms and planktonic cells, which matched 18 distinct proteins, including enolase and the elongation factor Tu. According to an RT-qPCR study, all 18 genes were downregulated in biofilms. Fourteen function and pathway-associated genes were reported using high-throughput sequencing and bioinformatics analysis. The 14 genes were upregulated in biofilm conditions and they may play important roles in bacterial defense, colonization, invasion, and virulence (<xref rid="ref210" ref-type="bibr">210</xref>). A comparative <italic>in silico</italic> study comparing the proteomes of <italic>B. melitensis</italic> Rev.1 and 16&#x2009;M was performed to identify biomarkers proteins, which distinguish the vaccine and the field strain. MALDI-TOF MS has identified two unique biomarkers most similar to ribosomal proteins (L24 and S12) (<xref rid="ref211" ref-type="bibr">211</xref>). For accurate identification of <italic>Brucella</italic> species in animals and humans, Elbehiry et al. have recognized the proteomics of <italic>Brucella</italic> species with the help of the MALDI-Biotyper (MBT) system and then confirmed the results by microfluidic electrophoresis. This strategy was proved to be effective for the diagnosis of <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens in animals and humans. Eleven positive <italic>B. melitensis</italic> isolates and 14 positive <italic>B. abortus</italic> isolates were reported (<xref rid="ref212" ref-type="bibr">212</xref>).</p>
              <p>Furthermore, Mahmud et al. studied the entire proteome of <italic>B. abortus</italic> strain 2,308 by using subtractive genomic analysis, and the results showed that only three membrane proteins (acriflavine resistance protein B, ABC transporter permease, and penicillin-binding protein 2) were observed to be potential novel vaccine candidates in cattle, while ABC transporter permease was predicted as a novel drug target for humans (<xref rid="ref213" ref-type="bibr">213</xref>). <italic>B. abortus</italic> and <italic>B. melitensis</italic> outbreak strains from cows and sheep, respectively, were subjected to label-free quantitative proteomic analysis, which identified 402 differentially expressed proteins. Among these, 63 and 103 proteins were only found in the whole-cell extracts of <italic>B. melitensis</italic> and <italic>B. abortus</italic> field strains, respectively (<xref rid="ref214" ref-type="bibr">214</xref>). Following numerous environmental pressures and adaptations, 1,221 differently expressed proteins in <italic>B. abortus</italic> were reported using label-free relative quantitative proteomics and mass spectrometry (<xref rid="ref215" ref-type="bibr">215</xref>).</p>
              <p>To investigate protein biomarkers for <italic>Brucella</italic> in human and livestock serum, a targeted multiple reactions monitoring-mass spectrometry (MRM-MS) method was created. In total, 30 synthetic peptides that match 10 immunodominant <italic>B. abortus</italic> proteins were created using bioinformatics analysis; and 117 serum samples from people and animals, classified as clinically confirmed (45), suspected (62), and control (10), were used to optimize the MRM-MS technique for the precise detection of these peptides. Transitions for four peptides were found in various clinically confirmed and suspected human and cattle serum samples using high-throughput MRM tests. Of them, peptide NAIYDVVTR with 100% specificity was repeatedly found in the clinically proved serum samples of both people and livestock, and it corresponds to the <italic>B. abortus</italic> protein (BruAb2_0537) (<xref rid="ref216" ref-type="bibr">216</xref>).</p>
              <p>With vaccine antigen prediction based on bioinformatics analysis of pathogen genomes, reverse vaccinology (RV) has been demonstrated to be a highly successful method (<xref rid="ref217" ref-type="bibr">217</xref>). Therefore, based on the <italic>Brucella</italic> protein-coding genome, RV has been utilized in several research projects to evaluate prospective vaccine candidates (<xref rid="ref218" ref-type="bibr">218</xref>). Zai et al. carried out a wide-scale study to screen putative antigens against 213 pathogenic strains of <italic>Brucella</italic> spp. with global geographic distribution using core proteome analysis and a compositive RV technique. Six biological characteristics (subcellular localization, antigen similarity, antigenicity, mature epitope density, pathogenicity, and adhesion probability) were used to rank candidate proteins. A total of 32 potential antigens were chosen for the RV analysis. Omp19 was used as a positive control, and T4SS protein VirB8 and type I secretion system (T1SS) protein HlyD were chosen for the evaluation of immunogenicity and preliminary protection in a mouse model (<xref rid="ref219" ref-type="bibr">219</xref>). In addition, Vishnu et al. revealed eight proteins as potential vaccine candidates after thoroughly screening the exoproteome and secretome of <italic>B. melitensis</italic> 16&#x2009;M. These proteins include the LPS-assembly protein LptD, heme transporter BhuA, hemagglutinin, flagellin FliC, 7-alpha-hydroxysteroid dehydrogenase, and immunoglobulin-binding protein EIBE. The functions of BhuA and hemagglutinin are crucial for the establishment of infection (<xref rid="ref220" ref-type="bibr">220</xref>).</p>
              <p>Moreover, Aslam et al. obtained the core genes set from 56 full genome sequences of <italic>B. melitensis</italic>. To find prospective proteins as drug targets and multi-epitope vaccine constructions from core genes, strict bioinformatics architecture of comparative genomics and RV techniques were used. Based on their function and specific metabolic pathways, lack of homology to human and human gut microbiome proteins, and potential for acting as drug targets, the 23 proteins were predicted as novel drug targets (<xref rid="ref221" ref-type="bibr">221</xref>). Furthermore, apolipoprotein N-acyltransferase (Int) was found to be the probable target of the most abundant short RNA released during early infection with bone marrow-derived macrophages (BMDMs) in <italic>B. abortus</italic>. Int expression was shown to be reduced during BMDM infection, and immunoinformatic analysis of the protein sequence allowed the reasonable selection of a potential immunogenic epitope that was investigated as a vaccine candidate (<xref rid="ref222" ref-type="bibr">222</xref>).</p>
              <p>Moreover, a multi-epitope vaccine (MEV) was designed using the RV technique of Omp25, Omp10, Omp31, and BtpB for creating MEV. In addition, 9 linear B-cell epitopes, 2 conformational B-cell epitopes, 13 cytotoxic T-lymphocyte (CTL) epitopes, and 17 helper T-lymphocyte (HTL) epitopes were predicted and proposed for further research. To increase immunogenicity, the vaccine peptide&#x2019;s N-terminal was supplemented with the proper adjuvants. A final MEV with 806 amino acids was created using linkers and adjuvants. The outcomes of the trials on animals showed that the MEV had a significant immunogenicity and could be used as a <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccine (<xref rid="ref223" ref-type="bibr">223</xref>). Small extracellular vesicles known as exosomes are released by cells and serve as a means of intercellular communication. Yi et al. investigated the extracellular interferon-inducible transmembrane protein 3 (IFITM3) role in the immunological response to <italic>Brucella</italic> infection. For the first time, they demonstrated that <italic>B. melitensis</italic> strain M5 can induce macrophages to release a significant amount of exosomes. The most significant finding was that the exosomes from <italic>Brucella</italic> M5-infected cells effectively reduced <italic>Brucella&#x2019;s</italic> intracellular survival. Additionally, animals developed <italic>Brucella</italic> antibodies after immunization with exosomes containing IFITM3, reducing spleen CFU and tissue damage. Overall, this provides novel ideas for the progress of candidate vaccines for <italic>Brucella</italic> (<xref rid="ref224" ref-type="bibr">224</xref>).</p>
            </sec>
          </sec>
        </sec>
      </sec>
      <sec id="sec17">
        <label>4.4.</label>
        <title>Basic techniques for <italic>Brucella</italic> diagnosis</title>
        <p>Classical serological tests, such as RBPT and tube agglutination, are simple, rapid, and inexpensive. On the other hand, they produce non-specific reactions with other Gram-negative bacteria, such as <italic>E. coli</italic> O157, <italic>Vibrio cholera</italic>, <italic>Francisella tularensis</italic>, and <italic>Yersinia enterocolitica</italic>, which have antigenic matches to <italic>Brucella</italic>. To avoid these reactions, ELISA tests, mainly iELISA, have been used as a confirmatory test for brucellosis detection. ELISA tests are characterized by the usage of different antigens, enzyme conjugates, and substrates (<xref rid="ref109" ref-type="bibr">109</xref>). CFT is a very specific test that can detect IgM and IgG1 antibodies. However, antibodies of the IgG2 type impede complement fixation resulting in false negative results (<xref rid="ref67" ref-type="bibr">67</xref>). A recent comparative serological assay study for brucellosis diagnosis has used 2,317 sera samples [sheep (<italic>n</italic> =&#x2009;552), goats (<italic>n</italic> =&#x2009;1,345), and cattle (<italic>n</italic> =&#x2009;420)]. The results indicated that 189 (8.2%), 191 (8.2%), and 48 (2.1%) tested positive using RBPT, iELISA, and CFT, respectively (<xref rid="ref225" ref-type="bibr">225</xref>). Nucleic acid tests combine the ability to identify the pathogen with the rapidity of molecular-based assays to provide great performance. In this concern, <italic>Brucella</italic> spp. has been detected using multiplexed PCR and loop-mediated isothermal amplification (LAMP). LAMP is a method of nucleic acid amplification that is significantly rapid with high sensitivity and specificity. In addition, it does not require expensive reagents or instruments, and so it aids in cost reduction (<xref rid="ref226" ref-type="bibr">226</xref>).</p>
      </sec>
    </sec>
    <sec id="sec18">
      <label>5.</label>
      <title>Future perspectives for controlling bovine brucellosis</title>
      <sec id="sec19">
        <label>5.1.</label>
        <title>Nanotechnology and recent trends for the diagnosis of bovine brucellosis</title>
        <p>The development of novel tools for <italic>Brucella</italic> diagnosis is urgently needed for eliminating rampant <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens in many parts of the world. Meanwhile, the existing methods for brucellosis diagnosis are time-consuming and expensive as they require a weary experimental procedure and sophisticated experimental devices. To overcome these shortcomings, it is truly necessary to establish real-time, on-site, sensitive, and rapid detection methods. For bovine brucellosis, Yang and co-workers investigated a simple, visible, specific, and reliable label-based polymer nanoparticles lateral flow immunoassay (LFIA) biosensor that can reduce the need for advanced equipment and simplify the detection technique. <italic>B. abortus</italic>-LAMP associated with nanoparticle-based LFIA targeting the BruAb2_0168 gene was established and performed successfully, and the technique demonstrated excellent sensitivity and specificity for the detection of many <italic>Brucella</italic> strains, including reference strains, vaccine strains, and field isolates (<xref rid="ref227" ref-type="bibr">227</xref>). For on-site diagnosis of human brucellosis, LFIA was designed for the first time as a signal probe depending on blue silica nanoparticles (SiNPs) for quick detection with good sensitivity and specificity (<xref rid="ref228" ref-type="bibr">228</xref>). Li et al. proved that the <italic>Brucella</italic> Multiple Cross Displacement Amplification-Lateral Flow Biosensor (MCDA-LFB) is a fast, easy, reliable, and sensitive method for detecting all <italic>Brucella</italic> spp. strains, and it can be utilized as a possible <italic>Brucella</italic> strain screening tool in many laboratories (<xref rid="ref229" ref-type="bibr">229</xref>).</p>
        <p>Nanotechnology has transformed the field of infectious disease diagnosis and the development of pharmaceutics (<xref rid="ref230" ref-type="bibr">230</xref>, <xref rid="ref231" ref-type="bibr">231</xref>). Their importance in biological applications stems from their reduced size and distinct physicochemical features, which enabled regulated drug release, targeted drug delivery, and <italic>in vivo</italic> immunomodulation. In terms of sensitivity, specificity, time, and cost, nanotechnology has been proved to be a more efficient diagnostic tool. Metal nanoparticles, polymeric nanoparticles, nanoemulsions, liposomes, and nanocrystals are among the nanomaterials employed in veterinary diagnostics and therapies (<xref rid="ref232" ref-type="bibr">232</xref>). Hybridization assays based on metal nanoparticles are simple to set up and can be read visually via a color shift caused by plasmonic interactions between the probes, so they are interesting approaches for diagnostic purposes. In particular, gold has been preferred in the application of these hybridization assays for the molecular detection of <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens. Usually, with the addition of simple reagents such as salts, gold causes a color shift from red to purple when aggregated (<xref rid="ref233" ref-type="bibr">233</xref>). Pal et al. have demonstrated the visual detection of <italic>Brucella</italic> in bovine biological samples using DNA-activated gold nanoparticles. They created a thiol-modified probe that was specific to a certain gene that codes for <italic>Brucella</italic> outer membrane protein. More importantly, they proved that the gold nanoparticle-based probe can be used for direct and simple visual identification of <italic>Brucella</italic> antigen from a wide range of bovine materials, including sperm, milk, and urine. Additionally, this test could be used to choose bulls for semen collection, to examine frozen semen before artificial insemination, or to examine bulk milk before packaging to ensure customer safety by decreasing exposure to <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens (<xref rid="ref234" ref-type="bibr">234</xref>).</p>
        <p>A rapid and affordable method for diagnosing human brucellosis has been created using a gold nanobiosensor based on the localized surface plasmon resonance (LSPR). LPS of <italic>B. melitensis</italic> and <italic>B. abortus</italic> were isolated and covalently attached to the surface of gold nanoparticles for this purpose. The cutoff value for detecting captured anti-<italic>Brucella</italic> antibodies was determined by measuring the redshift on the LSPR peak, which revealed a significant difference between the healthy and true positive patients. Moreover, for an accurate assessment of this method, 40 sera from true negative and positive patients were used for comparing its results with the culture (standard method), standard tube agglutination test, and anti-brucellosis IgM and IgG levels using ELISA. The LSPR-based technique was accurate with 85, 100, 100, and 86% in terms of sensitivity, specificity, positive predictive value, and negative predictive value, respectively (<xref rid="ref235" ref-type="bibr">235</xref>). In cattle, a comparative study was performed to evaluate the use of gold nanoparticles (GNPs) and quantum dots (QDs) as labels in the immunochromatographic serodiagnosis of brucellosis. For QDs, the results were highly sensitive in detecting specific antibodies against <italic>B. abortus</italic> LPS. So, the use of QDs in immunochromatographic serodiagnosis proved to be a good technique to increase the accuracy of immunochromatographic assay (ICA) (<xref rid="ref236" ref-type="bibr">236</xref>).</p>
        <p>The sensitivity and specificity for detecting <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens have recently been increased using biosensors. They can turn biological responses into electrical signals for analysis. A bioreceptor (antibodies, enzymes, nucleic acids, microbes, etc.) is combined with a physical transducer (optical, electrochemical, or mass-based) to generate a measurable signal (<xref rid="ref237" ref-type="bibr">237</xref>). Many new signal transduction systems based on nanoparticles have been developed to improve pathogen detection strategies (<xref rid="ref237" ref-type="bibr">237</xref>, <xref rid="ref238" ref-type="bibr">238</xref>). Sandwich immunoassay nanoparticle-based biosensors are regarded as a promising way to introduce selective and sensitive diagnostic tools (<xref rid="ref239" ref-type="bibr">239</xref>). Silica and magnetic nanoparticles have distinct properties linked to their hydrophobic surfaces and the capacity to modify their surfaces with chemical groups, and so they were used for visual and spectrophotometric detection of <italic>Brucella</italic> (<xref rid="ref240" ref-type="bibr">240&#x2013;242</xref>). An immunosensor was designed based on blue-SiNPs and paramagnetic nanoparticles (PMNPs). The synthesized immunosensor was conjugated with a polyclonal antibody against <italic>B. abortus</italic> and applied to the bacterial culture. Then, a magnet was used to create a sandwich structure of PMNPs <italic>B. abortus</italic>-blue-SiNPs. Afterward, the absorbance of the blue color produced by the silica structure was quantified using a spectrophotometer, combined with the visible color change, to determine the presence of bacterial cells in the samples (<xref rid="ref243" ref-type="bibr">243</xref>). Li et al. developed another immunosensor using the immune magnetic beads (IMBs) probe and the QDs staphylococcal protein A (SPA) probe. The IMB probe and serum were combined first to allow the <italic>Brucella</italic> antibodies to interact with <italic>Brucella</italic> outer membrane protein coated on the IMB probe surface. The fluorescence intensity of QDs was correlated with the quantity of anti-<italic>Brucella</italic> antibodies. For rapid and label-free detection of <italic>B. melitensis</italic> in milk and milk products, a new quartz crystal microbalance (QCM) aptasensor has been developed. On the QCM chip, the particular aptamer sequence linked with magnetic nanoparticles was immobilized to identify <italic>B. melitensis</italic> (<xref rid="ref244" ref-type="bibr">244</xref>). A rapid, accurate, simple, and sensitive fluorescent immunochromatographic strip test (ICST) based on a quantum dot fluorescent microsphere (QDFM) is another application for the field screening of brucellosis using animal serum.</p>
        <p>The impedance technique is another rapid and inexpensive alternative to label-free biosensor technologies. For quick detection of <italic>B. melitensis</italic>, Wu et al. developed a label-free impedance immunosensor based on gold nanoparticles with modified screen-printed carbon electrodes (GNP-SPCEs). The interaction of <italic>B. melitensis</italic> antigens on the surface of GNP-SPCEs with antibody molecules was studied using cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). In less than 1.5&#x2009;h, this biosensor was able to detect 1&#x2009;&#xD7;&#x2009;10<sup>4</sup> and 4&#x2009;&#xD7;&#x2009;10<sup>5</sup> CFU/ml of <italic>B. melitensis</italic> in pure culture and milk samples, respectively (<xref rid="ref245" ref-type="bibr">245</xref>). Zinc oxide nanoparticles (ZnO-NPs) are novel types of low-cost, low-toxicity nanomaterials. In this concern, a <italic>Brucella</italic> identification biosensor has been designed for detecting <italic>Brucella</italic> in milk samples (<xref rid="ref246" ref-type="bibr">246</xref>).</p>
      </sec>
      <sec id="sec20">
        <label>5.2.</label>
        <title>Nanotechnology for treatment of bovine brucellosis</title>
        <p>Many host immunological responses, such as lysosomal enzymes and oxidative burst (<xref rid="ref247" ref-type="bibr">247</xref>), cannot interact with the intracellular <italic>Brucella</italic>, and medications are unable to effectively target <italic>Brucella</italic>-infected cells as they lose their antimicrobial effectiveness in the intracellular environment (<xref rid="ref248" ref-type="bibr">248</xref>). In humans, routine treatment regimens for brucellosis by using two or more antibiotics are the most effective way to evade relapses occurring and prevent prolonged use of these drugs (<xref rid="ref249" ref-type="bibr">249</xref>). Khan et al. revealed that mutations in the rpoB, gyrA, and gyrB genes have been linked to antimicrobial resistance in rifampicin and ciprofloxacin (<xref rid="ref250" ref-type="bibr">250</xref>). Resistance to rifampin, ampicillin&#x2013;sulbactam, trimethoprim&#x2013;sulfamethoxazole, and ampicillin in <italic>B. melitensis</italic> was estimated at 36.36, 31.82, 27.27, and 22.70%, respectively. Resistance to rifampin, trimethoprim&#x2013;sulfamethoxazole, ampicillin, and ampicillin&#x2013;sulbactam was detected at 35.71, 32.14, 28.57, and 32.14% of <italic>B. abortus</italic> isolates, respectively (<xref rid="ref212" ref-type="bibr">212</xref>).</p>
        <p>Gene therapy, which is beneficial in the clinic, may provide an alternate path to a complete cure for brucellosis by assisting in the deletion or inactivation of genes involved in <italic>Brucella</italic> multiplication in host cells. To simulate the host microorganism interaction <italic>in vitro</italic>, ovine macrophages were infected with <italic>B. melitensis</italic> and then the infected cells were transduced with CRISPR/Cas9 lentiviral vectors targeting <italic>Brucella</italic> RNA polymerase subunit A (RpolA) or virulence-associated gene (virB10) at a multiplicity of infection (MOI) of 60. When infected cells were transduced with the RpolA vector, the number of internalized <italic>Brucella</italic> per cell was decreased significantly, whereas when macrophages were transduced with a conventional lentiviral vector expressing the green fluorescence protein, the number of internalized brucellae per cell remained unaffected. This highlights the bactericidal effect of the CRISPR/Cas9 system (<xref rid="ref251" ref-type="bibr">251</xref>).</p>
        <p>Niosomes are drug-delivery materials made from cholesterol and a non-ionic surfactant that self-assemble (<xref rid="ref252" ref-type="bibr">252</xref>, <xref rid="ref253" ref-type="bibr">253</xref>). They improve medication bioavailability by delaying drug release and changing drug half-lives, resulting in good drug accumulation within the targeted site (<xref rid="ref254" ref-type="bibr">254</xref>). Chitosan and genetically modified organisms (GMOs) are minimal in toxicity and highly compatible with biological systems (<xref rid="ref255" ref-type="bibr">255</xref>). The synergistic effect of chitosan/GMO is beneficial for drug targeting and maintenance by increasing the mucoadhesion characteristics of niosomes (<xref rid="ref254" ref-type="bibr">254</xref>, <xref rid="ref256" ref-type="bibr">256</xref>). Abo EL-Ela et al. showed that the combination of doxycycline (DOX)-loaded chitosan&#x2013;sodium alginate nanoparticles (<xref rid="ref257" ref-type="bibr">257</xref>, <xref rid="ref258" ref-type="bibr">258</xref>) with <italic>in situ</italic> pH-responsive curcumin-loaded niosome hydrogel for brucellosis treatment and splenic count reduction is an effective recipe. The successful production of DOX and curcumin as nanomaterials for the treatment of intracellular bacteria may improve human brucellosis therapy due to their prolonged release, stability, and high trapping efficiency. However, the injected antimicrobial drugs did not completely eliminate <italic>Brucella</italic> in the artificially infected Guinea pigs. The results showed a considerable drop in viable <italic>Brucella</italic> numbers in the spleen and blood (<xref rid="ref259" ref-type="bibr">259</xref>).</p>
        <p>Using RB51 phage lysates (RL) and S19 lysates (SL), researchers have described unique and successful immunotherapy for the treatment of bovine brucellosis in cows. The combination of these two phage lysates (RL and SL) was administered subcutaneously in a 2-mL dose, and blood samples were found to be <italic>Brucella</italic>-free even after 3&#x2009;months of phage cocktail immunization. RL elicited a stronger cell-mediated immune response than SL, while SL elicited a higher amount of humoral immunological response. The study&#x2019;s findings are encouraging, suggesting that bacteriophage lysates could be used to treat bovine brucellosis because the conventional treatment regimens are ineffective (<xref rid="ref260" ref-type="bibr">260</xref>).</p>
        <p>Another study has produced polyanhydride nanoparticles of copolymers of sebacic acid to encapsulate the antibiotics doxycycline and rifampicin, and the best antibacterial activity was reported at 72&#x2009;h and lasted up to a week after the nanoparticles released rifampicin for a week. In comparison with soluble drug controls, treatment of <italic>B. melitensis</italic>-infected macrophages with rifampicin-containing nanoparticles rapidly removed viable intracellular bacteria after 48&#x2009;h, and pretreatment with the nano-formulations prevented intracellular infection (<xref rid="ref261" ref-type="bibr">261&#x2013;263</xref>). Infected BALB/c mice were treated for 5&#x2009;days with a nanoparticle cocktail combining doxycycline and rifampicin, which reduced bacterial burden in the liver. Infected mice with <italic>B. melitensis</italic> were given either a daily 0.5&#x2009;mg doxycycline dose or a single 0.5&#x2009;mg doxycycline-encapsulated nanoparticles delivered once a week to demonstrate the <italic>in vivo</italic> extended antibiotic release. After 3&#x2009;weeks, bacterial numbers in the spleen and liver of animals treated with the weekly nano-formulation and other animals that received daily soluble medication were statistically equivalent, indicating a 7-fold dosage sparing. These findings showed that using nanotherapeutics to treat persistent bacterial infections increased antimicrobial efficacy and improved <italic>in vivo</italic> activity through a combination of intracellular transport, dosage sparing, and prolonged release (<xref rid="ref264" ref-type="bibr">264</xref>).</p>
      </sec>
      <sec id="sec21">
        <label>5.3.</label>
        <title>Methods for eradication of bovine brucellosis</title>
        <p>The major goal during the settlement of <italic>Brucella</italic> control programs is decreasing the spread of microorganisms. Hosein et al. carried out a study measuring the time needed to reach <italic>Brucella</italic>-free herd status. They found that 6&#x2009;months are required to reach free status, which is considered a long time, allowing infection to spread to other areas, especially under unsanitary conditions, a husbandry system that favors mixed populations of different ages, sex, aborted, and pregnant animals, and a lack of controlled animal movement. As a result, efficient animal brucellosis control needs surveillance to identify diseased animal herds, reservoir eradication, and vaccination of young heifers (<xref rid="ref64" ref-type="bibr">64</xref>). In Dohuk Governorate, Iraq, the usage of the Rev. 1 vaccine for all female and male animals older than 3&#x2009;months reduces the prevalence of brucellosis from 36 to 6.6% (<xref rid="ref23" ref-type="bibr">23</xref>). The results revealed that successful animal vaccination, gradual culling of seropositive cattle, and small ruminants through slaughter, environmental hygiene, and human personal protection all have a role in limiting disease spread in livestock and human populations (<xref rid="ref265" ref-type="bibr">265</xref>, <xref rid="ref266" ref-type="bibr">266</xref>), in addition to the eradication of carrier animals in the herd, such as dogs, cats, and mice, to eliminate infection sources (<xref rid="ref267" ref-type="bibr">267</xref>).</p>
        <p>Veterinary organizations should also use ongoing education and training initiatives to raise farmers&#x2019; knowledge and understanding of prevention methods and transmission routes as an important principle (<xref rid="ref91" ref-type="bibr">91</xref>, <xref rid="ref268" ref-type="bibr">268</xref>). It is also worth noting that brucellosis risks are higher in dairy farms that use artificial insemination or natural breeding with non-certified brucellosis bulls, so it is important to use semen from certified free farms (<xref rid="ref97" ref-type="bibr">97</xref>).</p>
        <p>In the point of test and slaughter in most developed countries, culling of the suspected or reactor animals based on serology is practiced in addition to immunization. In most developing countries, this technique is not implemented due to financial constraints and the lack of healthcare infrastructure. A production strategy that avoids direct or indirect contact with diseased neighboring farms and/or contaminated feed or pasture is also essential as a part of the control plan (<xref rid="ref33" ref-type="bibr">33</xref>). When the rate of infection is reduced to an acceptable level, a test and slaughter strategy could be implemented. So, if the disease prevalence is quite low (1 to 4%), then eradication of the disease in a short-to-medium timeframe could be achieved using a test and slaughter eradication program. At the second level, if the prevalence is low to moderate (5&#x2013;10%) then eradication of the disease in a medium-long timescale could be achieved by simultaneous vaccination of young replacements as well as testing and slaughter of seropositive adult animals. At the third level, if there is a high rate of occurrence (10%), then the mass vaccination program is the only way to control the disease, regardless of the level of professional organization or financial resources available (<xref rid="ref29" ref-type="bibr">29</xref>). Moreover, Pasteurization renders <italic>B. abortus</italic> inactive, and its survival outside the host is primarily dependent on environmental factors. <italic>Brucella</italic> can live for 2&#x2013;3&#x2009;months in wet soil, up to 8&#x2009;months in an aborted fetus in the shade, 1&#x2013;2&#x2009;months in dry soil, 3&#x2013;4&#x2009;months in fecal matter, and up to 8&#x2009;months in liquid sewage tanks. Bacterial viability is prolonged at cold temperatures, and organisms can survive in the frozen cadaver for many years (<xref rid="ref269" ref-type="bibr">269</xref>). Raw or undercooked animal items (even bone marrow) and unpasteurized dairy products should not be ingested (<xref rid="ref270" ref-type="bibr">270</xref>).</p>
      </sec>
    </sec>
    <sec sec-type="conclusions" id="sec22">
      <label>6.</label>
      <title>Conclusion</title>
      <p>Brucellosis is regarded as one of the most hazardous pathogens and seriously affects humans and terrestrial animals. It is still a serious threat to animals and humans despite all the measures taken by the WHO and veterinary authorities with a significant economic effect all over the world. In the current study, we provided an updated insight into the worldwide <italic>Brucella</italic> distribution, possible predisposing factors incorporated in emerging <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens, immune status and different types of <italic>Brucella</italic> vaccines (subunit vaccines, vector vaccines, and genetic engineering vaccines), genomics and proteomics approaches, and future perspectives for prevention and control of bovine brucellosis, which undoubtedly will be used as a platform for controlling <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens in many endemic countries; this will help scientists, veterinarians, and animals&#x2019; owners to prevent introducing <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens to the free areas. More specifically, many risk and predisposing factors have been summarized, including the control of fetal fluids, placenta, dogs and cats, and animal movements as the introduction of new animals to herds without certification. Moreover, pasteurization of dairy products should be done and watched as it is an important source of transmitting the disease to humans. The veterinary authorities should educate farmers to be aware of the dangers of the disease, how it can be transmitted between animals and humans, and how they can protect themselves. An important section of this review has discussed the novel techniques based on nanotechnology and genetic engineering of the antigenic structures of the pathogen to make an easy, simple, and proper diagnosis. Finally, we conclude that following many comprehensive studies in the field of genomics and proteomics of <italic>Brucella</italic> pathogens, vaccination proved to be effective for defeating these pathogens in the endemic areas as well as preventing disease introduction to the other free areas of the world.</p>
    </sec>
    <sec id="sec23">
      <title>Author contributions</title>
      <p>ASD: Conceptualization, Data curation, Writing &#x2013; original draft, Writing &#x2013; review &amp; editing.
